<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Neutron</span></h1>

<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>

<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Subatomic particle with no charge</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the subatomic particle. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Neutron_(disambiguation)" title="Neutron (disambiguation)">Neutron (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">Neuron</a> or <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">Neutrino</a>.</div>

<p>The <b>neutron</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Subatomic_particle" title="Subatomic particle">subatomic particle</a>, symbol <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> or <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, which has a neutral (not positive or negative) charge, and a <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> slightly greater than that of a <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a>. Protons and neutrons constitute the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nuclei</a> of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>. Since protons and neutrons behave similarly within the nucleus, and each has a mass of approximately one <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">atomic mass unit</a>, they are both referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nuc_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nuc-7">[7]</a></sup> Their properties and interactions are described by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_physics" title="Nuclear physics">nuclear physics</a>.  Protons and neutrons are not <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary particles</a>; each is composed of three <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>. 
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_properties" title="Chemical properties">chemical properties</a> of an atom are mostly determined by the configuration of <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> that orbit the atom's heavy nucleus. The electron configuration is determined by the charge of the nucleus, which is determined by the number of protons, or <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>. The number of neutrons is the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_number" title="Neutron number">neutron number</a>. Neutrons do not affect the electron configuration, but the sum of atomic and neutron numbers is the mass of the nucleus.
</p><p>Atoms of a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> that differ only in neutron number are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotopes" title="Isotopes">isotopes</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, with atomic number 6, has an abundant isotope <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> with 6 neutrons and a rare isotope <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">carbon-13</a> with 7 neutrons.  Some elements occur in nature with only one <a href="/wiki/Stable_nuclide" title="Stable nuclide">stable isotope</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>; Other elements occur with many stable isotopes, such as <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> with ten stable isotopes, and some elements such as <a href="/wiki/Technetium" title="Technetium">technetium</a> have no stable isotope.
</p><p>The properties of an atomic nucleus depend on both atomic and neutron numbers. With their positive charge, the protons within the nucleus are repelled by the long-range <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">electromagnetic force</a>, but the much stronger, but short-range, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a> binds the nucleons closely together. Neutrons are required for the stability of nuclei, with the exception of the single-proton <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> nucleus. Neutrons are produced copiously in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">fusion</a>. They are a primary contributor to the <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">nucleosynthesis</a> of chemical elements within <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> through fission, fusion, and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_capture" title="Neutron capture">neutron capture</a> processes.
</p><p>The neutron is essential to the production of nuclear power. In the decade after the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_neutron" title="Discovery of the neutron">neutron was discovered</a> by <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> in 1932,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chad1932_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chad1932-8">[8]</a></sup> neutrons were used to induce many different types of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_transmutation" title="Nuclear transmutation">nuclear transmutations</a>. With the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> in 1938,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> it was quickly realized that, if a fission event produced neutrons, each of these neutrons might cause further fission events, in a cascade known as a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_chain_reaction" title="Nuclear chain reaction">nuclear chain reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ENW_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-ENW-10">[10]</a></sup> These events and findings led to the first self-sustaining <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chicago_Pile-1" title="Chicago Pile-1">Chicago Pile-1</a>, 1942) and the first <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Trinity_(nuclear_test)" title="Trinity (nuclear test)">Trinity</a>, 1945).
</p><p>Dedicated <a href="/wiki/Neutron_source" title="Neutron source">neutron sources</a> like <a href="/wiki/Neutron_generator" title="Neutron generator">neutron generators</a>, <a href="/wiki/Research_reactor" title="Research reactor">research reactors</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spallation" title="Spallation">spallation sources</a> produce free neutrons for use in <a href="/wiki/Irradiation" title="Irradiation">irradiation</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">neutron scattering</a> experiments. A free neutron spontaneously decays to a proton, an <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>, and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antineutrino" title="Antineutrino">antineutrino</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Exponential_decay#Mean_lifetime" title="Exponential decay">mean lifetime</a> of about 15 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPP_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-RPP-11">[11]</a></sup> Free neutrons do not directly ionize atoms, but they do indirectly cause <a href="/wiki/Ionizing_radiation#Neutrons" title="Ionizing radiation">ionizing radiation</a>, so they can be a biological hazard, depending on dose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ENW_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-ENW-10">[10]</a></sup> A small natural "neutron background" flux of free neutrons exists on Earth, caused by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Air_shower_(physics)" title="Air shower (physics)">showers</a>, and by the natural radioactivity of spontaneously fissionable elements in the <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)#Earth's_crust" title="Crust (geology)">Earth's crust</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatNeu_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatNeu-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

.navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Nuclear_physics" title="Template:Nuclear physics"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Nuclear_physics" title="Template talk:Nuclear physics"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Nuclear_physics&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a> is formed by a number of protons, <i>Z</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>), and a number of neutrons, <i>N</i> (the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_number" title="Neutron number">neutron number</a>), bound together by the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a>. The atomic number determines the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical properties</a> of the atom, and the neutron number determines the <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> or <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ENW_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-ENW-10">[10]</a></sup> The terms isotope and nuclide are often used <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonymously</a>, but they refer to chemical and nuclear properties, respectively. Isotopes are nuclides with the same atomic number, but different neutron number. Nuclides with the same neutron number, but different atomic number, are called <a href="/wiki/Isotone" title="Isotone">isotones</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_number" title="Atomic mass number">atomic mass number</a>, <i>A</i>, is equal to the sum of atomic and neutron numbers. Nuclides with the same atomic mass number, but different atomic and neutron numbers, are called <a href="/wiki/Isobar_(nuclide)" title="Isobar (nuclide)">isobars</a>.
</p><p>The nucleus of the most common <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> (with the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbol</a> <sup>1</sup>H) is a lone proton. The nuclei of the heavy hydrogen isotopes <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a> (D or <sup>2</sup>H) and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> (T or <sup>3</sup>H) contain one proton bound to one and two neutrons, respectively. All other types of atomic nuclei are composed of two or more protons and various numbers of neutrons. The most common nuclide of the common chemical element <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <sup>208</sup>Pb, has 82 protons and 126 neutrons, for example. The <a href="/wiki/Table_of_nuclides" title="Table of nuclides">table of nuclides</a> comprises all the known nuclides. Even though it is not a chemical element, the neutron is included in this table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>The free neutron has a mass of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008939565413300000♠"></span>939<span style="margin-left:.25em;">565</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">413</span>.3 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt#Mass" title="Electronvolt">eV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></a></span>, or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6973167492747100000♠"></span>1.674<span style="margin-left:.25em;">927</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">471</span><span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−27</sup> <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kg</a></span>, or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100866491588000♠"></span>1.008<span style="margin-left:.25em;">664</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">915</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">88</span> <a href="/wiki/Dalton_(unit)" title="Dalton (unit)">Da</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_CODATA_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-2014_CODATA-4">[4]</a></sup> The neutron has a mean square <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> of about <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984800000000000000♠"></span>0.8<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−15</sup> <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">m</a></span>, or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6984800000000000000♠"></span>0.8 <a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Povh_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Povh-14">[14]</a></sup> and it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-%C2%BD" title="Spin-½">spin-½</a> <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">fermion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basdevant2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basdevant2-15">[15]</a></sup> The neutron has no measurable electric charge. With its positive electric charge, the proton is directly influenced by <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric fields</a>, whereas the neutron is unaffected by electric fields. But the neutron has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_magnetic_moment" title="Neutron magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a>, so the neutron is influenced by <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>. The neutron's magnetic moment has a negative value, because its orientation is opposite to the neutron's spin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Llewellyn_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Llewellyn-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>A free neutron is unstable, <a href="/wiki/Free_neutron_decay" title="Free neutron decay">decaying</a> to a proton, electron and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antineutrino" title="Antineutrino">antineutrino</a> with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of just under 15 minutes (<span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002879600000000000♠"></span>879.6<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.8 s</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDG_Live:_2020_Review_of_Particle_Physics_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-PDG_Live:_2020_Review_of_Particle_Physics-5">[5]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, is possible because the mass of the neutron is slightly greater than that of the proton. The free proton is stable. However, neutrons or protons bound in a nucleus can be stable or unstable, depending on the <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a>. Beta decay, in which neutrons decay to protons, or vice versa, is governed by the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak force</a>, and it requires the emission or absorption of electrons and neutrinos, or their antiparticles.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fission.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="309" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nuclear_fission.svg/150px-Nuclear_fission.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nuclear_fission.svg/225px-Nuclear_fission.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Nuclear_fission.svg/300px-Nuclear_fission.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nuclear_fission.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nuclear fission caused by absorption of a neutron by uranium-235. The heavy nuclide fragments into lighter components and additional neutrons.</div></div></div>
<p>Protons and neutrons behave almost identically under the influence of the nuclear force within the nucleus. The concept of <a href="/wiki/Isospin" title="Isospin">isospin</a>, in which the proton and neutron are viewed as two quantum states of the same particle, is used to model the interactions of nucleons by the nuclear or weak forces. Because of the strength of the nuclear force at short distances, the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> of nucleons is more than seven orders of magnitude larger than the electromagnetic energy binding electrons in atoms. <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">Nuclear reactions</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>) therefore have an <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> that is more than ten million times that of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a>. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">mass–energy equivalence</a>, nuclear binding energies reduce the mass of nuclei. Ultimately, the ability of the nuclear force to store energy arising from the electromagnetic repulsion of nuclear components is the basis for most of the energy that makes nuclear reactors or bombs possible. In nuclear fission, the absorption of a neutron by a heavy nuclide (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a>) causes the nuclide to become unstable and break into light nuclides and additional neutrons. The positively charged light nuclides then repel, releasing electromagnetic <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">potential energy</a>.
</p><p>The neutron is classified as a <i><a href="/wiki/Hadron" title="Hadron">hadron</a></i>, because it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_particle" title="Composite particle">composite particle</a> made of <a href="/wiki/Quark" title="Quark">quarks</a>. The neutron is also classified as a <i><a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryon</a></i>, because it is composed of three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Valence_quark" title="Valence quark">valence quarks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The finite size of the neutron and its magnetic moment both indicate that the neutron is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composite_particle" title="Composite particle">composite</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Elementary_particle" title="Elementary particle">elementary</a>, particle. A neutron contains two <a href="/wiki/Down_quark" title="Down quark">down quarks</a> with charge −<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span><a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge"><i>e</i></a> and one <a href="/wiki/Up_quark" title="Up quark">up quark</a> with charge +<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">2</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">3</span></span><i>e</i>.
</p><p>Like protons, the quarks of the neutron are held together by the <a href="/wiki/Strong_interaction" title="Strong interaction">strong force</a>, mediated by <a href="/wiki/Gluon" title="Gluon">gluons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cottingham_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cottingham-18">[18]</a></sup> The nuclear force results from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force#The_nuclear_force_as_a_residual_of_the_strong_force" title="Nuclear force">secondary effects of the more fundamental strong force</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_neutron" title="Discovery of the neutron">Discovery of the neutron</a></div>
<p>The story of the discovery of the neutron and its properties is central to the extraordinary developments in atomic physics that occurred in the first half of the 20th century, leading ultimately to the atomic bomb in 1945. In the 1911 <a href="/wiki/Rutherford_model" title="Rutherford model">Rutherford model</a>, the atom consisted of a small positively charged massive nucleus surrounded by a much larger cloud of negatively charged electrons. In 1920, <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a> suggested that the nucleus consisted of positive protons and neutrally charged particles, suggested to be a proton and an electron bound in some way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BakLec_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-BakLec-19">[19]</a></sup> Electrons were assumed to reside within the nucleus because it was known that <a href="/wiki/Beta_particle" title="Beta particle">beta radiation</a> consisted of electrons emitted from the nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BakLec_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-BakLec-19">[19]</a></sup> 
About the time Rutherford suggested the neutral proton-electron composite, several other publications appeared making similar suggestions, and in 1921 the American chemist <a href="/wiki/William_Draper_Harkins" title="William Draper Harkins">W.D. Harkins</a> first named the hypothetical particle a "neutron".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeathHist_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FeathHist-21">[21]</a></sup> The name derives from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> root for <i>neutralis</i> (neuter) and the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> suffix <i>-on</i> (a suffix used in the names of subatomic particles, i.e. <i>electron</i> and <i>proton</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPT_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPT-23">[23]</a></sup> References to the word <i>neutron</i> in connection with the atom can be found in the literature as early as 1899, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FeathHist_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-FeathHist-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the 1920s, physicists assumed that the atomic nucleus was composed of protons and "nuclear electrons"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FK_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FK-25">[25]</a></sup> but there were obvious problems. It was difficult to reconcile the proton–electron model for nuclei with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisenberg_uncertainty_relation" title="Heisenberg uncertainty relation">Heisenberg uncertainty relation</a> of quantum mechanics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuewer_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuewer-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pais_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pais-27">[27]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Klein_paradox" title="Klein paradox">Klein paradox</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> discovered by <a href="/wiki/Oskar_Klein" title="Oskar Klein">Oskar Klein</a> in 1928, presented further quantum mechanical objections to the notion of an electron confined within a nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuewer_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stuewer-26">[26]</a></sup> Observed properties of atoms and molecules were inconsistent with the nuclear spin expected from the proton–electron hypothesis. Both protons and electrons carry an intrinsic spin of <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span><i>ħ</i>. Isotopes  of the same species (i.e. having the same number of protons) can have both integer or fractional spin, i.e. the neutron spin must be also fractional (<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1050945101" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="sfrac tion" role="math"><span class="num">1</span><span class="sr-only">/</span><span class="den">2</span></span><i>ħ</i>). But there is no way to arrange the spins of an electron and a proton (supposed to bond to form a neutron) to get the fractional spin of a neutron.
</p><p>In 1931, <a href="/wiki/Walther_Bothe" title="Walther Bothe">Walther Bothe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbert_Becker_(physicist)" title="Herbert Becker (physicist)">Herbert Becker</a> found that if <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particle</a> radiation from <a href="/wiki/Polonium" title="Polonium">polonium</a> fell on <a href="/wiki/Beryllium" title="Beryllium">beryllium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, an unusually penetrating radiation was produced. The radiation was not influenced by an electric field, so Bothe and Becker assumed it was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_radiation" title="Gamma radiation">gamma radiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The following year <a href="/wiki/Ir%C3%A8ne_Joliot-Curie" title="Irène Joliot-Curie">Irène Joliot-Curie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Joliot-Curie" title="Frédéric Joliot-Curie">Frédéric Joliot-Curie</a> in Paris showed that if this "gamma" radiation fell on <a href="/wiki/Paraffin_wax" title="Paraffin wax">paraffin</a>, or any other <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>-containing compound, it ejected protons of very high energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Neither Rutherford nor <a href="/wiki/James_Chadwick" title="James Chadwick">James Chadwick</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_Laboratory" title="Cavendish Laboratory">Cavendish Laboratory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> were convinced by the gamma ray interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Chadwick quickly performed a series of experiments that showed that the new radiation consisted of uncharged particles with about the same mass as the proton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chad1932_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chad1932-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> These particles were neutrons. Chadwick won the 1935 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">Nobel Prize in Physics</a> for this discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1935_Nobel_Prize_in_Physics_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-1935_Nobel_Prize_in_Physics-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png/360px-Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png/540px-Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png/720px-Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blausen_0342_ElectronEnergyLevels.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Models depicting the nucleus and electron energy levels in hydrogen, helium, lithium, and neon atoms. In reality, the diameter of the nucleus is about 100,000 times smaller than the diameter of the atom.</div></div></div>
<p>Models for an atomic nucleus consisting of protons and neutrons were quickly developed by <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The proton–neutron model explained the puzzle of nuclear spins. The origins of beta radiation were explained by <a href="/wiki/Enrico_Fermi" title="Enrico Fermi">Enrico Fermi</a> in 1934 by the <a href="/wiki/Fermi%27s_interaction" title="Fermi's interaction">process of beta decay</a>, in which the neutron decays to a proton by <i>creating</i> an electron and a (at the time undiscovered) neutrino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-40">[40]</a></sup> In 1935, Chadwick and his doctoral student <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Goldhaber" title="Maurice Goldhaber">Maurice Goldhaber</a> reported the first accurate measurement of the mass of the neutron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1934, Fermi had bombarded heavier elements with neutrons to induce radioactivity in elements of high atomic number. In 1938, Fermi received the Nobel Prize in Physics "for his demonstrations of the existence of new radioactive elements produced by neutron irradiation, and for his related discovery of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reactions</a> brought about by slow neutrons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper-43">[43]</a></sup> In 1938 <a href="/wiki/Otto_Hahn" title="Otto Hahn">Otto Hahn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lise_Meitner" title="Lise Meitner">Lise Meitner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fritz_Strassmann" title="Fritz Strassmann">Fritz Strassmann</a> discovered <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a>, or the fractionation of uranium nuclei into light elements, induced by neutron bombardment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hahn_1958_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hahn_1958-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In 1945 Hahn received the 1944 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> "for his discovery of the fission of heavy atomic nuclei".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel1944_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel1944-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NF-1944press_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-NF-1944press-49">[49]</a></sup> The discovery of nuclear fission would lead to the development of nuclear power and the atomic bomb by the end of World War II.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Beta_decay_and_the_stability_of_the_nucleus">Beta decay and the stability of the nucleus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Beta decay and the stability of the nucleus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_stability" title="Valley of stability">Valley of stability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beta-decay_stable_isobars" title="Beta-decay stable isobars">Beta-decay stable isobars</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_emission" title="Neutron emission">Neutron emission</a></div>
<p>Since interacting protons have a mutual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interaction" title="Electromagnetic interaction">electromagnetic repulsion</a> that is stronger than their attractive <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear interaction</a>, neutrons are a necessary constituent of any atomic nucleus that contains more than one proton (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diproton" title="Diproton">diproton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron%E2%80%93proton_ratio" title="Neutron–proton ratio">neutron–proton ratio</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Neutrons bind with protons and one another in the nucleus via the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_force" title="Nuclear force">nuclear force</a>, effectively moderating the repulsive forces between the protons and stabilizing the nucleus.
</p><p>The neutrons and protons bound in a nucleus form a quantum mechanical system wherein each nucleon is bound in a particular, hierarchical quantum state.  Protons can decay to neutrons, or vice versa, within the nucleus.  This process, called <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, requires the emission of an electron or <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> and an associated <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrino</a>.  These emitted particles carry away the energy excess as a nucleon falls from one quantum state to a lower energy state, while the proton (or neutron) changes to a neutron (or proton). Such decay processes can occur only if allowed by basic energy conservation and quantum mechanical constraints.  The stability of nuclei depends on these constraints.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_neutron_decay">Free neutron decay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Free neutron decay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Free_neutron_decay" title="Free neutron decay">Free neutron decay</a></div>
<p>Outside the nucleus, free neutrons are unstable and have a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean_lifetime" title="Mean lifetime">mean lifetime</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002879600000000000♠"></span>879.6<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.8 s</span> (about 14 minutes, 40 seconds); therefore the <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> for this process (which differs from the mean lifetime by a factor of <span class="texhtml"><a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">ln</a>(2) = 0.693</span>) is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002610100000000000♠"></span>610.1<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.7 s</span> (about 10 minutes, 10 seconds).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPP_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-RPP-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> This decay is only possible because the mass of the proton is less than that of the neutron.  By the mass-energy equivalence, when a neutron decays to a proton this way it attains a lower energy state.  Beta decay of the neutron, described above, can be denoted by the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="texhtml"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">ν</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>where <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, and <span class="texhtml"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">ν</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></span> denote the proton, electron and electron antineutrino, respectively.
For the free neutron the <a href="/wiki/Decay_energy" title="Decay energy">decay energy</a> for this process (based on the masses of the neutron, proton, and electron) is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987125345155936904♠"></span>0.782<span style="margin-left:.25em;">343</span> <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">MeV</a></span>. The maximal energy of the beta decay electron (in the process wherein the neutrino receives a vanishingly small amount of kinetic energy) has been measured at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987125290201283400♠"></span>0.782<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>0.013 MeV</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The latter number is not well-enough measured to determine the comparatively tiny rest mass of the neutrino (which must in theory be subtracted from the maximal electron kinetic energy) as well as neutrino mass is constrained by many other methods.
</p><p>A small fraction (about one in 1000) of free neutrons decay with the same products, but add an extra particle in the form of an emitted gamma ray:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="texhtml"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">ν</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>γ<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>This gamma ray may be thought of as an "internal <a href="/wiki/Bremsstrahlung" title="Bremsstrahlung">bremsstrahlung</a>" that arises from the electromagnetic interaction of the emitted beta particle with the proton. Internal bremsstrahlung gamma ray production is also a minor feature of beta decays of bound neutrons (as discussed below).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beta-minus_Decay.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="170" data-file-width="250" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beta-minus_Decay.svg/360px-Beta-minus_Decay.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beta-minus_Decay.svg/540px-Beta-minus_Decay.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Beta-minus_Decay.svg/720px-Beta-minus_Decay.svg.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beta-minus_Decay.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Schematic" title="Schematic">schematic</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus of an atom</a> indicating <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>β<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> radiation, the emission of a fast electron from the nucleus (the accompanying antineutrino is omitted). In the Rutherford model for the nucleus, red spheres were protons with positive charge and blue spheres were protons tightly bound to an electron with no net charge.<br/> The <b>inset</b> shows beta decay of a free neutron as it is understood today; an electron and antineutrino are created in this process.</div></div></div>
<p>A very small minority of neutron decays (about four per million) are so-called "two-body (neutron) decays", in which a proton, electron and antineutrino are produced as usual, but the electron fails to gain the <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6982217896002232000♠"></span>13.6 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">eV</a></span> necessary energy to escape the proton (the <a href="/wiki/Ionization_energy" title="Ionization energy">ionization energy</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>), and therefore simply remains bound to it, as a neutral <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a> (one of the "two bodies"). In this type of free neutron decay, almost all of the neutron <a href="/wiki/Decay_energy" title="Decay energy">decay energy</a> is carried off by the antineutrino (the other "body"). (The hydrogen atom recoils with a speed of only about (decay energy)/(hydrogen rest energy) times the speed of light, or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7005250000000000000♠"></span>250 <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">km/s</a></span>.)
</p><p>The transformation of a free proton to a neutron (plus a positron and a neutrino) is energetically impossible, since a free neutron has a greater mass than a free proton. But a high-energy collision of a proton and an electron or neutrino can result in a neutron.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bound_neutron_decay">Bound neutron decay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Bound neutron decay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">Atomic nucleus</a></div>
<p>While a free neutron has a half life of about <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002612000000000000♠"></span>10.2 min</span>, most neutrons within nuclei are stable. According to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell_model" title="Nuclear shell model">nuclear shell model</a>, the protons and neutrons of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclide</a> are a <a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_quantum_mechanics" title="Introduction to quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical system</a> organized into discrete <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">energy levels</a> with unique <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_numbers" title="Quantum numbers">quantum numbers</a>. For a neutron to decay, the resulting proton requires an available state at lower energy than the initial neutron state. In stable nuclei the possible lower energy states are all filled, meaning they are each occupied by two protons with <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> up and spin down. The <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a> therefore disallows the decay of a neutron to a proton within stable nuclei. The situation is similar to electrons of an atom, where electrons have distinct <a href="/wiki/Atomic_orbital" title="Atomic orbital">atomic orbitals</a> and are prevented from decaying to lower energy states, with the emission of a <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a>, by the exclusion principle.
</p><p>Neutrons in unstable nuclei can decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a> as described above. In this case, an energetically allowed quantum state is available for the proton resulting from the decay. One example of this decay is <a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">carbon-14</a> (6 protons, 8 neutrons) that decays to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen-14" title="Nitrogen-14">nitrogen-14</a> (7 protons, 7 neutrons) with a half-life of about <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7011180825048000000♠"></span>5,730 years</span>.
</p><p>Inside a nucleus, a proton can transform into a neutron via <a href="/wiki/Inverse_beta_decay" title="Inverse beta decay">inverse beta decay</a>, if an energetically allowed quantum state is available for the neutron. This transformation occurs by emission of a positron and an electron neutrino:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="texhtml"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>ν<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>The transformation of a proton to a neutron inside of a nucleus is also possible through <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="texhtml"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>p<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>e<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> → <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>n<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">0</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>ν<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">e</sub></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Positron capture by neutrons in nuclei that contain an excess of neutrons is also possible, but is hindered because positrons are repelled by the positive nucleus, and quickly <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">annihilate</a> when they encounter electrons.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition_of_beta_decay_types">Competition of beta decay types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Competition of beta decay types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Three types of beta decay in competition are illustrated by the single isotope <a href="/wiki/Copper-64" title="Copper-64">copper-64</a> (29 protons, 35 neutrons), which has a half-life of about 12.7 hours. This isotope has one unpaired proton and one unpaired neutron, so either the proton or the neutron can decay. This particular nuclide is almost equally likely to undergo proton decay (by <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a>, 18% or by <a href="/wiki/Electron_capture" title="Electron capture">electron capture</a>, 43%) or neutron decay (by electron emission, 39%).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Decay_of_the_neutron_by_elementary_particle_physics">Decay of the neutron by elementary particle physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Decay of the neutron by elementary particle physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beta_Negative_Decay.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Beta_Negative_Decay.svg/200px-Beta_Negative_Decay.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Beta_Negative_Decay.svg/300px-Beta_Negative_Decay.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Beta_Negative_Decay.svg/400px-Beta_Negative_Decay.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beta_Negative_Decay.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Feynman_diagram" title="Feynman diagram">Feynman diagram</a> for beta decay of a neutron into a proton, electron, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_antineutrino" title="Electron antineutrino">electron antineutrino</a> via an intermediate heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W_boson" title="W boson">W boson</a></div></div></div>
<p>Within the theoretical framework of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">Standard Model</a> for particle physics, the neutron is composed of two down quarks and an up quark. The only possible decay mode for the neutron that <a href="/wiki/Conservation_law" title="Conservation law">conserves</a> <a href="/wiki/Baryon_number" title="Baryon number">baryon number</a> is for one of the neutron's quarks to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavour_changing_processes" title="Flavour changing processes">change</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavour_(physics)" title="Flavour (physics)">flavour</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">weak interaction</a>. The decay of one of the neutron's down quarks into a lighter up quark can be achieved by the emission of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W_boson" title="W boson">W boson</a>. By this process, the Standard Model description of beta decay, the neutron decays into a proton (which contains one down and two up quarks), an electron, and an <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron antineutrino</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Electron_Capture_Decay.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="310" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Electron_Capture_Decay.svg/220px-Electron_Capture_Decay.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Electron_Capture_Decay.svg/330px-Electron_Capture_Decay.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Electron_Capture_Decay.svg/440px-Electron_Capture_Decay.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Electron_Capture_Decay.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The leading-order <a href="/wiki/Feynman_diagram" title="Feynman diagram">Feynman diagram</a> for <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>β<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> decay of a proton into a neutron, positron, and <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrino</a> via an intermediate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W_boson" title="W boson"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>W<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> boson</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The decay of the proton to a neutron occurs similarly through the electroweak force. The decay of one of the proton's up quarks into a down quark can be achieved by the emission of a W boson. The proton decays into a neutron, a positron, and an electron neutrino.  This reaction can only occur within an atomic nucleus which has a quantum state at lower energy available for the created neutron.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Intrinsic_properties">Intrinsic properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Intrinsic properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mass">Mass</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mass">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The mass of a neutron cannot be directly determined by <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> since it has no electric charge. But since the masses of a proton and of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuteron" title="Deuteron">deuteron</a> can be measured with a mass spectrometer, the mass of a neutron can be deduced by subtracting proton mass from deuteron mass, with the difference being the mass of the neutron plus the <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> of deuterium (expressed as a positive emitted energy). The latter can be directly measured by measuring the energy (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle B_{d}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>B</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mi>d</mi>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle B_{d}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="B_d" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8152ba18cface59883b0d177b0295a249b6433db" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.856ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>) of the single <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987356324050708800♠"></span>2.224 MeV</span> gamma photon emitted when a deuteron is formed by a proton capturing a neutron (this is exothermic and happens with zero-energy neutrons). The small recoil kinetic energy (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle E_{rd}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m_{n}=m_{d}-m_{p}+B_{d}-E_{rd}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle m_{n}=m_{d}-m_{p}+B_{d}-E_{rd}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle m_{n}=m_{d}-m_{p}+B_{d}-E_{rd}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b25ebc2172709ad86c63e99073aba04756179fcf" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:27.515ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>The energy of the gamma ray can be measured to high precision by X-ray diffraction techniques, as was first done by Bell and Elliot in 1948. The best modern (1986) values for neutron mass by this technique are provided by Greene, et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> These give a neutron mass of:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>m</i><sub>neutron</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100864490400000♠"></span>1.008<span style="margin-left:.25em;">644</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">904</span>(14) <a href="/wiki/Dalton_(unit)" title="Dalton (unit)">Da</a></span></dd></dl>
<p>The value for the neutron mass in MeV is less accurately known, due to less accuracy in the known conversion of <a href="/wiki/Dalton_(unit)" title="Dalton (unit)">Da</a> to MeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><i>m</i><sub>neutron</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7002939565630000000♠"></span>939.565<span style="margin-left:.25em;">63</span>(28) <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt#Mass" title="Electronvolt">MeV/<i>c</i><sup>2</sup></a></span>.</dd></dl>
<p>Another method to determine the mass of a neutron starts from the beta decay of the neutron, when the momenta of the resulting proton and electron are measured.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_charge">Electric charge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Electric charge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The total electric charge of the neutron is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="5000000000000000000♠"></span>0 <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge"><i>e</i></a></span>. This zero value has been tested experimentally, and the present experimental limit for the charge of the neutron is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="3021799999999999999♠"></span>−2(8)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−22</sup> <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge"><i>e</i></a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PDGLIVE_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-PDGLIVE-6">[6]</a></sup> or <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="3040700000000000000♠"></span>−3(13)<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>−41</sup> <a href="/wiki/Coulomb" title="Coulomb">C</a></span>. This value is consistent with zero, given the experimental <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty#Measurements" title="Uncertainty">uncertainties</a> (indicated in parentheses). By comparison, the charge of the proton is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000100000000000000♠"></span>+1 <a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge"><i>e</i></a></span>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_moment">Magnetic moment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neutron&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Magnetic moment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_magnetic_moment" title="Neutron magnetic moment">Neutron magnetic moment</a></div>
<p>Even though the neutron is a neutral particle, the magnetic moment of a neutron is not zero. The neutron is not affected by electric fields, but it is affected by magnetic fields. The magnetic moment of the neutron is an indication of its quark substructure and internal charge distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-56">[56]</a></sup>
The value for the neutron's magnetic moment was first directly measured by <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis Alvarez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Felix_Bloch" title="Felix Bloch">Felix Bloch</a> at <a href="/wiki/Berkeley,_California" title="Berkeley, California">Berkeley, California</a>, in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez-57">[57]</a></sup> Alvarez and Bloch determined the magnetic moment of the neutron to be <span class="nowrap"><i>μ</i><sub>n</sub>= <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="2999807000000000000♠"></span>−1.93(2) <i>μ</i><sub>N</sub></span></span>, where <i>μ</i><sub>N</sub> is the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magneton" title="Nuclear magneton">nuclear magneton</a>.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Quark_model" title="Quark model">quark model</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadrons" title="Hadrons">hadrons</a>, the neutron is composed of one up quark (charge +2/3 <i>e</i>) and two down quarks (charge −1/3 <i>e</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-56">[56]</a></sup> The magnetic moment of the neutron can be modeled as a sum of the magnetic moments of the constituent quarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perk_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Perk-58">[58]</a></sup> The calculation assumes that the quarks behave like pointlike Dirac particles, each having their own magnetic moment. Simplistically, the magnetic moment of the neutron can be viewed as resulting from the vector sum of the three quark magnetic moments, plus the orbital magnetic moments caused by the movement of the three charged quarks within the neutron.
</p><p>In one of the early successes of the Standard Model in 1964 Mirza A.B. Beg, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_W._Lee" title="Benjamin W. Lee">Benjamin W. Lee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Pais" title="Abraham Pais">Abraham Pais</a> theoretically calculated the ratio of proton to neutron magnetic moments to be −3/2, which agrees with the experimental value to within 3%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenberg_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenberg-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beg_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beg-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sakita_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sakita-61">[61]</a></sup> The measured value for this ratio is <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="2999854010195000000♠"></span>−1.459<span style="margin-left:.25em;">898</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">05</span>(34)</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_CODATA_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-2014_CODATA-4">[4]</a></sup> A contradiction of the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> basis of this calculation with the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a>, led to the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Color_charge" title="Color charge">color charge</a> for quarks by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_W._Greenberg" title="Oscar W. Greenberg">Oscar W. Greenberg</a> in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenberg_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenberg-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The above treatment compares neutrons with protons, allowing the complex behavior of quarks to be subtracted out between models, and merely exploring what the effects would be of differing quark charges (or quark type). Such calculations are enough to show that the interior of neutrons is very much like that of protons, save for the difference in quark composition with a down quark in the neutron replacing an up quark in the proton.
</p><p>The neutron magnetic moment can be roughly computed by assuming a simple <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">nonrelativistic</a>, quantum mechanical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wavefunction" title="Wavefunction">wavefunction</a> for <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">baryons</a> composed of three quarks.  A straightforward calculation gives fairly accurate estimates for the magnetic moments of neutrons, protons, and other baryons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Perk_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Perk-58">[58]</a></sup> For a neutron, the end result of this calculation is that the magnetic moment of the neutron is given by <span class="nowrap"><i>μ</i><sub>n</sub>= 4/3 <i>μ</i><sub>d</sub> − 1/3 <i>μ</i><sub>u</sub></span>, where <i>μ</i><sub>d</sub> and <i>μ</i><sub>u</sub> are the magnetic moments for the down and up quarks, respectively. This result combines the intrinsic magnetic moments of the quarks with their orbital magnetic moments, and assumes the three quarks are in a particular, dominant quantum state.
</p>
